[0:0:400,335] Natural  

[1:0:379,366] Resources  

[2:0:344,409] Natural Resources Agency programs protect and  

[2:1:344,418] restore California's diverse natural and cultural  

[2:2:344,428] resources for current and future generations,  

[3:0:344,438] including operating state parks, managing water  

[3:1:344,448] supplies, supporting forest health, encouraging  

[3:2:344,458] clean energy, and preserving fish and wildlife  

[3:3:344,468] habitats, rich farmlands and mineral resources.  

[3:4:344,478] Agency programs also protect the public through  

[3:5:344,488] suppression of wildfires and construction of levees  

[3:6:344,498] in the Central Valley. These programs not only  

[3:7:344,508] contribute to the state's unique quality of life, but  

[3:8:344,517] also are critical to sustaining a vibrant economy.

[1:0:242,43] 3100   Exposition Park  

[2:0:66,64] The Science Center, the Office of Exposition Park Management and the California African American Museum (CAAM), are  

[2:1:66,74] located in Exposition Park, a 160-acre tract in south Los Angeles, which is owned by the state. For budget purposes, these  

[2:2:66,84] three departments are collectively known as Exposition Park.  

[3:0:66,105] The mission of the Science Center is to stimulate curiosity and inspire science related learning. The Office of Exposition Park  

[3:1:66,115] Management provides long-term leadership in the development and implementation of park usage policy and day-to-day  

[3:2:66,125] management, operation and promotion of the park for its tenants and the public. The mission of CAAM is to research, collect,  

[3:3:66,135] preserve and interpret for public enrichment, the history, art and culture of African Americans with a multi-layered focus that is  

[3:4:66,146] global, national, and local with special emphasis on California and the western United States.

[6:0:66,294] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[7:0:66,312] 2300 - CALIFORNIA SCIENCE CENTER  

[8:0:66,332] Attracting over 2 million guests annually, the California Science Center represents one of California's premier educational and  

[8:1:66,342] family destinations. Governed by a nine-member Board of Directors appointed by the Governor, the Science Center develops  

[8:2:66,353] and features award-winning exhibits and internationally renowned education programs.  

[9:0:66,373] Hands-on educational exhibits and programs focus on science, math, technology, and conservation which explore biological  

[9:1:66,383] processes of humans, animals, plants, the Earth's ecosystems, habitats, and geophysical processes, as well as engineering,  

[9:2:66,393] communications, and transportation, on land and in space. In 2012, the Space Shuttle Endeavour was moved to its temporary  

[9:3:66,404] home - the Samuel Oschin Pavilion - while design and construction of the Science Center's Air and Space Center - its  

[9:4:66,414] permanent home - is completed.  

[10:0:66,434] In addition, the Science Center operates a 3D IMAX theater which features science related films and documentaries. The  

[10:1:66,444] Center for Science Learning offers professional development programs to improve math and science skills of teachers and  

[10:2:66,455] other educators. The Science Center School is a K-5 science, math and technology focused neighborhood charter school. As a  

[10:3:66,465] Title I school, it serves one of the more underserved and economically challenged communities in South Los Angeles. The  

[10:4:66,475] school's instructional programs and teacher training meet California science standards.  

[11:0:66,495] The Science Center's mission values accessibility and inclusiveness and strives to inspire interest in science among those  

[11:1:66,506] traditionally underrepresented in science, math, technology and engineering. The Science Center receives significant funding  

[11:2:66,516] support for exhibit development and capital outlay projects from the California Science Center Foundation.  

[12:0:66,534] 2305 - EXPOSITION PARK MANAGEMENT  

[13:0:66,551] The Office of Exposition Park Management (OEPM) is responsible for public safety operations, parking services management  

[13:1:66,561] and overall park beautification maintenance. In addition, OEPM administers supervision and coordination of park-wide events,  

[13:2:66,572] and facilitates communication efforts between state, city, county, and private entities represented within the park. OEPM is  

[13:3:66,582] responsible for the negotiations of ground leases and agreements which impact the state's interest, including development of  

[13:4:66,592] park facilities and future infrastructure improvements. OEPM is also responsible for managing the Exposition Park  

[13:5:66,602] Improvement Fund and generating revenue to maintain the park. Exposition Park enriches the internal park community and in  

[13:6:66,612] addition provides green space and recreational areas for the surrounding neighborhood.  

[14:0:66,630] 2310 - CALIFORNIA AFRICAN AMERICAN MUSEUM  

[15:0:66,648] The California African American Museum (CAAM) is governed by a seven-member board of directors appointed by the  

[15:1:66,658] Governor. CAAM's mission is history, art and culture. The diverse exhibits are displayed on a seasonal basis in the various  

[15:2:66,668] galleries of the 44,000 square foot building. CAAM delivers its mission to the public through its history and art collections;  

[15:3:66,678] permanent, self-curated, temporary and traveling exhibits; and, innumerable programs and workshops, lectures, seminars,  

[15:4:66,689] films, educational services, standards-based curriculums, and cultural presentations. CAAM facilitates the delivery of these  

[15:5:66,699] services through dozens of outreach efforts including school bus services, docent led tours and creative workshops, through  

[15:6:66,709] Buses & Docents; CAAM Technology Arts Program; employment skill development for high school students under Young  

[16:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[0:0:66,13] 2019-20 STATE BUDGET — RES   10   NATURAL RESOURCES 

[1:0:201,43] 3125   California Tahoe Conservancy  

[2:0:66,64] The California Tahoe Conservancy's mission is to lead California's efforts to restore and enhance the extraordinary natural and  

[2:1:66,74] recreational resources of the Lake Tahoe Basin (Basin). The Conservancy achieves its mission by developing and  

[2:2:66,84] implementing programs that acquire environmentally sensitive land, provide grants or directly fund soil erosion prevention and  

[2:3:66,95] watershed restoration, and improve stream environments, wildlife habitats, forest health, and water quality. The Conservancy  

[2:4:66,105] also balances these activities with programs to improve public access and recreational opportunities in an environmentally  

[2:5:66,115] sound manner by acquiring lakefront property, constructing pedestrian and bike paths, and building public access facilities.  

[3:0:66,135] The Conservancy is also a key implementing agency of the Environmental Improvement Program (EIP) which is intended to  

[3:1:66,146] restore and protect the Basin's natural resources.   The EIP leverages funding from the states of California and Nevada, the  

[3:2:66,156] Tahoe Regional Planning Agency, the federal government, local governments, and various private entities for projects needed  

[3:3:66,166] to achieve environmental goals in the Basin. The EIP is updated periodically to include more refined estimates of projects,  

[3:4:66,176] modifications in the scope of identified projects, and inclusion of new projects. 

[4:0:66,197] Because the Conservancy's programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the Conservancy has a capital outlay program  

[4:1:66,207] to support this need. For the specifics on the Conservancy's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview."

[9:0:66,564] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[9:1:66,583] 2340 - TAHOE CONSERVANCY  

[10:0:66,600] The Conservancy owns and manages nearly 4,700 parcels totaling more than 6,500 acres, including thousands of quarter-acre  

[10:1:66,610] lots within the Basin’s urban areas. These parcels provide open space, water quality and recreational benefits, and have  

[10:2:66,621] significantly reduced the potential level of development in the Basin. The Conservancy manages its lands for multiple benefits  

[10:3:66,631] and inspects each parcel once every two years to monitor forest health and to identify maintenance needs and encroachments.  

[10:4:66,641] Maintaining healthy forests in urban areas to help protect life and property constitutes another fundamental role of the  

[10:5:66,651] Conservancy’s land management program. The Conservancy also manages 11 properties with recreational amenities,  

[10:6:66,661] including six beaches that provide public access to Lake Tahoe.  

[11:0:66,679] The Conservancy and its partners are also increasingly shifting to large landscape, multi-jurisdictional, multiple-benefit projects  

[11:1:66,689] to more rapidly and comprehensively achieve restoration goals in the Basin. Working at a landscape scale makes it easier for  

[11:2:66,700] the Conservancy and its partners to integrate the planning for and management of multiple values and resources across  

[11:3:66,710] jurisdictions. The Conservancy plans to continue working with a wide variety of partners to restore the health and resilience of  

[12:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[0:0:66,13] 2019-20 STATE BUDGET — RES   12 

[1:0:454,13] NATURAL RESOURCES 

[2:0:166,32] 3125   California Tahoe Conservancy - Continued 

[3:0:66,69] the Basin’s forests and watersheds, with the aim of also increasing the pace and scale of restoration. This includes improving  

[3:1:66,79] forest diversity, protecting wildlife, applying beneficial fire, clearing meadows of encroaching trees, reducing streambank  

[3:2:66,89] erosion, reconnecting floodplains, and treating storm water. This work will enhance the ability of these lands to respond to  

[3:3:66,99] wildfire, drought, insects, and climate change. This evolution in the Conservancy’s approach to planning also aligns it with  

[3:4:66,109] national and statewide trends toward restoring landscape resilience.  

[4:0:66,127] The Conservancy also works closely with local governments to meet its mission by providing grants for acquisitions and site  

[4:1:66,137] improvements such as building roadside and storm drainage facilities and sediment basins, building bike trails, and creating  

[4:2:66,148] public gathering spaces and improving public access to the Lake.  

[4:3:66,165] 2345 - ACQUISITION AND SITE IMPROVEMENT PROJECTS  

[5:0:66,186] This program acquires environmentally sensitive lands and provides public access and recreation by enhancing existing  

[5:1:66,196] facilities and establishing new public access sites along the Lake and in natural areas. The program also preserves a wide  

[5:2:66,206] variety of habitats supporting endangered, threatened or rare animal and plant species, and restores streams and watersheds  

[5:3:66,216] such as marshes, meadows and riparian areas to mitigate development damage and enhance water quality.  

[6:0:66,234] In addition, the program is helping to reduce greenhouse gas emissions and achieve environmental benefits by promoting  

[6:1:66,244] urban in-fill development in the Basin. These efforts include selling, leasing, or exchanging non-environmentally sensitive  

[6:2:66,254] Conservancy lands and associated development rights to support revitalization efforts consistent with local area plans and town  

[6:3:66,265] center objectives.   Property exchanges and the proceeds from the sale or leasing of property are then used to (1) acquire other  

[6:4:66,275] environmentally sensitive lands or (2) fund conservation and other projects that support the Conservancy in fulfilling its mission.

[11:0:66,501] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW  

[12:0:66,519] The California Tahoe Conservancy manages programs to help protect Lake Tahoe's water quality and conserve wildlife habitat,  

[12:1:66,529] watershed areas, and public access on the California side of the Lake Tahoe Basin. The Conservancy is a primary implementer  

[12:2:66,539] of the State of California's responsibilities under the Environmental Improvement Program (EIP) for the Lake Tahoe Basin - a  

[12:3:66,550] 1997 agreement between California, Nevada, the Tahoe Regional Planning Agency, the federal government, local  

[12:4:66,560] governments, and various private entities. The EIP is updated periodically to include more refined estimates of projects,  

[12:5:66,570] modifications in the scope of identified projects, and new projects.

[1:0:188,43] 3210   Environmental Protection Program  

[1:1:66,64] The California Environmental Protection Program receives revenue from the sale of personalized motor vehicle license plates  

[1:2:66,74] by the Department of Motor Vehicles. Revenues are deposited in the California Environmental License Plate Fund. Funds  

[1:3:66,84] expended pursuant to Division 13.5 of the Public Resources Code must have one or more of the following purposes:  

[1:4:65,102] • The control and abatement of air pollution, including all phases of research into the sources, dynamics and effects of  

[1:5:76,112] environmental pollutants.  

[2:0:65,129] • The acquisition, preservation, restoration, or any combination thereof, of natural areas or ecological reserves.  

[2:1:65,145] • Environmental education, including formal school programs and informal public education programs.  

[2:2:65,161] • Protection of nongame species and threatened and endangered plants and animals.  

[2:3:65,177] • Protection, enhancement, and restoration of fish and wildlife habitat and related water quality, including review of the  

[2:4:76,187] potential impact of development activities and land use changes on that habitat.  

[2:5:65,204] • The purchase, on an opportunity basis, of real property consisting of sensitive natural areas for the state park system and for  

[2:6:76,214] local and regional parks.  

[3:0:65,230] • Reduction or minimization of the effects of soil erosion and the discharge of sediment into the waters of the Lake Tahoe  

[3:1:76,240] region, including the restoration of disturbed wetlands and stream environment zones.  

[4:0:66,268] The appropriations and expenditures for programs and projects funded from the California Environmental License Plate Fund  

[4:1:66,278] are contained in the budgets of the various state boards, commissions and departments.

[1:0:200,43] 3340   California Conservation Corps  

[2:0:66,64] The California Conservation Corps (CCC) provides young women and men the opportunity to work hard responding to fires,  

[2:1:66,74] floods and other disasters, restoring California's environment, and installing clean energy and energy conservation measures at  

[2:2:66,84] public facilities throughout the state. Through their service, the corpsmembers gain life, work, and academic skills to become  

[2:3:66,95] strong workers and citizens.  

[3:0:66,115] In addition to the CCC, there are also 14 local conservation corps located in metropolitan communities throughout the state that  

[3:1:66,125] are annually certified by the CCC, and engage young people in conservation, recycling, education, and training activities.  

[4:0:66,146] Because the   CCC's programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the CCC has a capital outlay program to support this  

[4:1:66,156] need. For the specifics on the CCC's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview."

[13:5:76,685] provide specialized training and assistance to corpsmembers to create educational and career pathways to forestry and

[7:0:66,582] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[7:1:66,600] 2360 - TRAINING AND WORK PROGRAM  

[7:2:66,618] This Program focuses on four areas:  

[8:0:66,635] Natural Resource Work: Corpsmembers protect and enhance the state's natural resources through park development, trail  

[8:1:66,645] construction, tree planting, fire hazard reduction, watershed improvement, wildlife habitat enhancement, removal of non-native  

[8:2:66,656] vegetation, meadow restoration, energy and water auditing and retrofitting, irrigation system installation, and drought-tolerant  

[8:3:66,666] and other landscaping.  

[9:0:66,684] Disaster Response: Corpsmembers are dispatched to fires, assisting with initial attack, mop-up and logistical support; floods,  

[9:1:66,694] filling sandbags, reinforcing levees and stabilizing hillsides; earthquakes, removing hazards and staffing disaster assistance  

[9:2:66,704] centers; oil spill clean-up; snow removal; search and rescue operations; pest infestation eradication; and homeland security  

[9:3:66,714] assistance.

[4:2:66,117] planning. The CCC also offers training in trail building, first aid, Hazardous Waste Operations and Emergency Response, and  

[4:3:66,127] firefighting certification, which can lead to internship opportunities with various employers in California.

[12:0:66,608] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW  

[13:0:66,626] The California Conservation Corps (CCC) operates 26 facilities statewide, including 9 residential and 17 non-residential  

[13:1:66,636] facilities in both urban and rural areas. These facilities enable the CCC to achieve its mission of providing employment, training,  

[13:2:66,646] and educational opportunities to young men and women. The facilities are also critical in the CCC assisting federal, state and  

[13:3:66,656] local agencies, as well as nonprofit entities, with the conservation of California's natural resources.  

[13:4:66,674] The CCC residential facilities house 80-100 corpsmembers and operate 24 hours a day, bringing young people together from  

[13:5:66,684] across the state, increasing capacity for emergency response and conservation projects. Non-residential centers range in size  

[13:6:66,695] from 30-60 corpsmembers and allow young people with families to live at home and report to work.  

[14:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[1:0:105,43] 3360   Energy Resources Conservation and Development Commission  

[2:0:66,64] The Energy Resources Conservation and Development Commission (Energy Commission) is responsible for ensuring a  

[3:0:66,74] reliable supply of energy to meet state needs while protecting public health, safety, and the environment. Activities include the  

[3:1:66,84] following: making public policy recommendations regarding energy; collecting targeted energy data and ensuring data is  

[3:2:66,95] managed responsibly; developing and implementing research, development, demonstration, and deployment policies and  

[3:3:66,105] programs that promote strategic energy investments; ensuring energy facilities approved by the Energy Commission are  

[4:0:66,115] designed, constructed, operated, and decommissioned in a manner that protects the environment and public health and safety,  

[4:1:66,125] and in compliance with all applicable laws, ordinances, regulations, and standards; adopting building and appliance efficiency  

[4:2:66,135] standards; promoting development and deployment of advanced transportation technology; and supporting climate change  

[4:3:66,146] goals through integrated resource planning, renewable energy development, and system integration.

[13:0:66,629] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[13:1:66,647] 2380 - REGULATORY and PLANNING PROGRAM  

[14:0:66,675] The Regulatory and Planning program facilitates markets in providing adequate statewide energy supplies. The program  

[14:1:66,686] certifies power plant sites, designates transmission line corridors, and monitors new energy facilities and fuel infrastructure for  

[14:2:66,696] compliance and supply and distribution adequacy consistent with state energy policies. Additionally, the Energy Assessments  

[14:3:66,706] program develops long-range projections for future energy supplies and demand, maintains current information on statewide  

[15:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[2:0:70,32] 3360   Energy Resources Conservation and Development Commission - Continued 

[3:0:66,69] electrical generation sources, consumption and peak demand, natural gas, transportation fuels, and distributed generation. The  

[3:1:66,79] program develops state energy policy based on the economic, financial, security, safety, and environmental implications of  

[3:2:66,89] supply, demand, and price/cost assessments, and monitors energy markets for competitive prices and potential market abuses.  

[4:0:66,107] 2380010 - Power Plant Site Certification and Transmission Line Corridor Designation Program 

[5:0:66,124] The Power Plant Site Certification and Transmission Line Corridor Designation programs are responsible for the following:  

[5:1:66,135] reviewing thermal power plant siting applications 50 megawatts and larger; ensuring power plant developers comply with  

[5:2:66,145] conditions of certification and all applicable laws and regulations when constructing, operating and closing power plants, and  

[5:3:66,155] investigating potential violations; reviewing petitions to amend siting decisions; reviewing applications for transmission line  

[5:4:66,165] corridor designation; evaluating constraints and opportunities for energy resource development; and analyzing reliability,  

[5:5:66,175] efficiency, and environmental performance issues related to power plants and the state electricity transmission grid.  

[6:0:66,211] 2380019 - Electricity Analysis  

[7:0:66,229] The Electricity Analysis program is responsible for evaluating current and future electricity supply market trends and  

[7:1:66,239] infrastructure needs; estimating costs and emissions of various generation types; assessing the adequacy of electricity  

[7:2:66,249] supplies; and analyzing regulations and environmental issues related to electricity. Additionally, this program addresses the  

[7:3:66,259] distribution system and its relationship to distributed generation, including planning and interconnection barriers and provides  

[7:4:66,269] information and recommendations to state agencies, electricity market participants, and the public. The natural gas program  

[7:5:66,280] component is responsible for evaluating current and future natural gas market trends and infrastructure needs, assessing the  

[7:6:66,290] adequacy of natural gas supplies, monitoring the interaction of natural gas and electricity to maintain system reliability,  

[7:7:66,300] analyzing regulations and environmental issues related to natural gas, and leading interagency work groups to monitor short-

[7:8:66,310] term natural gas market trends. The program also collects, monitors, and analyzes data on transportation fuel supply and  

[7:9:66,320] production, fuel infrastructure issues, and responds to energy and fuel shortages and emergencies.  

[8:0:66,356] 2385 - ENERGY RESOURCES CONSERVATION PROGRAM  

[9:0:66,384] The Energy Resources Conservation program objectives include reducing overall energy use and decreasing peak electricity  

[9:1:66,394] demand by identifying energy efficiency opportunities and developing and implementing programs and standards for efficiently  

[9:2:66,404] using energy in all sectors.  

[10:0:66,422] 2385010 - Building and Appliances Program  

[11:0:66,450] The Building and Appliances program objectives include reducing consumer and business energy use, water use, and peak  

[11:1:66,460] demand by developing and implementing strategies and minimum efficiency standards for appliances, equipment, and new and  

[11:2:66,470] existing residential and nonresidential buildings. The Building and Appliances program provides technical assistance on  

[11:3:66,480] building standards and enforcement support to the building industry and building departments. Further, the program maintains  

[11:4:66,490] a database of efficient appliances and equipment, and enforces compliance with the appliance efficiency standards.  

[12:0:66,508] 2385019 - Energy Projects Evaluation and Assistance Program  

[13:0:66,536] This program is responsible for providing technical assistance, grants, and loans to improve energy efficiency and reduce  

[13:1:66,546] operating costs of schools, colleges, local jurisdictions, hospitals, and public care facilities.  

[14:0:66,564] 2385028 - Demand Analysis  

[15:0:66,592] The Demand Analysis program is responsible for collecting and analyzing electricity and natural gas consumption data used to  

[15:1:66,602] prepare energy demand forecasts, forecasting peak and total energy consumption by sector, and estimating the amount of  

[15:2:66,612] energy conserved by existing and proposed program activities. The program conducts detailed electricity and natural gas end-

[15:3:66,622] use surveys which serve as a primary data source for energy demand forecasts, conservation potential studies, and market  

[15:4:66,633] and policy impact assessments. The program also performs transportation energy demand forecasting, conducts consumer  

[15:5:66,643] vehicle choice surveys, and assesses the impacts of transportation electrification on California electricity use.  

[16:0:66,678] 2390 - DEVELOPMENT PROGRAM  

[17:0:66,696] The Development Program focuses on transportation and alternatives to conventional fossil fuels. The mission is to support  

[17:1:66,706] provision of adequate and reliable transportation energy to the California transportation sector while balancing economic, public  

[18:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[3:0:66,69] health, safety, and environmental consequences. The program also conducts research, development, demonstration,  

[3:1:66,79] commercialization, and deployment activities on energy efficiency, renewable and advanced energy technologies, and  

[3:2:66,89] alternative and renewable fuel and advanced vehicle technologies so that future energy supplies are cost effective, secure, and  

[3:3:66,99] reliable; enhance environmental quality; and promote state and local economic development. The program provides technical  

[3:4:66,109] assistance, financial assistance, direct technology research and demonstration, technology forecasting, technology analysis  

[3:5:66,120] and evaluation, and information transfer.  

[3:6:66,137] 2390010 - Transportation Technology and Fuels  

[4:0:66,165] The Transportation Technology and Fuels program provides financial incentives to develop and deploy innovative technologies  

[4:1:66,175] and strategies that transform California's fuel and vehicle types to help attain the state's climate change policies. The program  

[4:2:66,186] goal is the development and deployment of low-carbon alternative fuels and advanced vehicle technologies in the marketplace,  

[4:3:66,196] without adopting any one preferred fuel or technology. The program provides analyses and recommendations to guide state  

[4:4:66,206] energy policy and legislation to achieve climate change and air quality objectives, reduce petroleum consumption, and promote  

[4:5:66,216] economic development. The program also provides incentives to workforce development and training that intersect with the  

[4:6:66,226] respective program goals.  

[5:0:66,244] 2390019 - Research and Development  

[6:0:66,272] The Research and Development program provides analysis and investment recommendations for research, demonstration,  

[6:1:66,282] and development innovations addressing current and emerging energy system requirements. The program supports projects  

[6:2:66,292] that complement other private or public sector investments. The program goal is to develop, and help bring to market, energy  

[6:3:66,303] solutions providing increased environmental benefits, greater system reliability, and lower energy costs. Benefits are provided  

[6:4:66,313] to Californians through investments in energy efficiency and demand response, advanced generation, energy storage,  

[6:5:66,323] strategies to reduce or mitigate energy-related environmental impact, transportation technologies, and energy transmission and  

[6:6:66,333] distribution system improvements.  

[6:7:66,351] 2390028 - Renewable Energy  

[7:0:66,379] The Renewable Energy program fosters growth of the renewable energy market by providing financial incentives to support the  

[7:1:66,389] development and use of eligible renewable energy products; requiring utilities to disclose information to consumers on their  

[7:2:66,399] power fuel mix; implementing the Renewables Portfolio Standard; and establishing solar equipment rating standards and  

[7:3:66,409] maintaining solar equipment lists meeting the performance and safety standards.  

[7:4:66,427] 2395 - LOAN REPAYMENT PROGRAM  

[8:0:66,455] The Loan Repayment program consists of Conservation and Development program loan repayments deposited into the  

[8:1:66,465] following accounts: State Energy Conservation Assistance Account; Local Jurisdiction Energy Assistance Account; Energy  

[8:2:66,475] Technologies Research, Development and Demonstration Account; Local Government Geothermal Resources Revolving  

[8:3:66,486] Subaccount; and Clean and Renewable Energy Business Financing Revolving Loan Fund.

[1:0:188,43] 3460   Colorado River Board of California  

[2:0:66,64] The Colorado River Board protects California's rights and interests in the water and power resources of the Colorado River  

[2:1:66,74] system. The Board works with: Colorado River Basin states (Arizona, California, Colorado, Nevada, New Mexico, Utah, and  

[2:2:66,84] Wyoming), federal agencies, other state agencies, six local agencies (Palo Verde Irrigation District, Imperial Irrigation District,  

[2:3:66,95] Coachella Valley Water District, Metropolitan Water District of Southern California, San Diego County Water Authority, Los  

[2:4:66,105] Angeles Department of Water and Power), Congress, the courts, and Mexico. Its activities include analyses of engineering,  

[2:5:66,115] legal and economic matters concerning the Colorado River resources of the seven basin states and the 1944 United States-

[2:6:66,125] Mexico Water Treaty obligation to deliver Colorado River water to Mexico.

[0:0:66,13] NATURAL RESOURCES 

[1:0:412,13] 2019-20 STATE BUDGET — RES   57 

[2:0:171,32] 3480   Department of Conservation - Continued 

[3:0:66,69] 2420 - GEOLOGIC HAZARDS AND MINERAL RESOURCES CONSERVATION  

[4:0:66,89] This program evaluates, assesses and maps the state's geologic and seismologic hazards, such as earthquakes, landslides,  

[4:1:66,99] tsunami and volcanic eruption threats, and hazardous minerals exposures, in order to protect the public health and safety and  

[4:2:66,109] the natural environment; analyzes the state's mineral assets and maps its mineral resources. Information is used by federal,  

[4:3:66,120] state, and local government agencies, industries and individual businesses, and the public to make informed decisions about  

[4:4:66,130] land use, seismic safety, and mineral development.  

[4:5:66,148] 2425 - OIL, GAS, AND GEOTHERMAL RESOURCES  

[5:0:66,165] This program regulates the drilling, operation, and abandonment of oil, natural gas, and geothermal wells to protect the  

[5:1:66,175] environment, prevent pollution, and ensure public safety. The state is fully reimbursed for program expenditures by annual  

[5:2:66,186] assessments and fees on the respective industries. Approximately 450 companies operate over 88,000 wells in California for  

[5:3:66,196] the production of oil, natural gas, and geothermal resources.  

[5:4:66,214] 2430 - LAND RESOURCE PROTECTION  

[6:0:66,231] This program protects agricultural farmland and open space through various financial incentives. Under the Land Conservation  

[6:1:66,241] Act, landowners who agree to keep their property undeveloped for at least ten years receive lower property tax assessments.  

[6:2:66,252] The California Farmland Conservancy Program provides grants to local governments and nonprofit land trusts for the  

[6:3:66,262] acquisition of agricultural conservation easements that permanently remove development rights, and therefore development  

[6:4:66,272] pressure, from agricultural lands. The Farmland Mapping and Monitoring Program within the Land Resource Protection  

[6:5:66,282] Program develops maps, statistics, and reports relating to farmland conversion, farmland inventory, and land protection to  

[6:6:66,292] assist in local land use decisions.  

[6:7:66,310] 2435 - MINE RECLAMATION  

[7:0:66,328] This program regulates active surface mining operations and monitors local lead agencies to ensure compliance with the  

[7:1:66,338] Surface Mining and Reclamation Act of 1975. It assists cities, counties, state agencies, and mine operators in their efforts to  

[7:2:66,348] reclaim mined lands to beneficial uses. This program also compiles an inventory of the state's estimated 47,000 abandoned  

[7:3:66,358] mines and remediates abandoned mine hazards to protect public safety.  

[7:4:66,376] 2440 - STATE MINING AND GEOLOGY BOARD  

[8:0:66,394] The Board serves as a regulatory and policy body for the state's geology, geologic and seismologic hazards, conservation of  

[8:1:66,404] mineral resources, and reclamation of mined lands. The Department's California Geological Survey and the Office of Mine  

[8:2:66,414] Reclamation provide the engineering, technical expertise, and support functions for the reports, plans, and maps which the  

[8:3:66,424] Board approves. The Board also serves as an appeals body for mining operations that have been issued notices of violations  

[8:4:66,435] or orders to comply, and disputes with local lead agencies.  

[9:0:66,472] DETAILED EXPENDITURES BY PROGRAM  †  

[10:0:418,496] 2017-18* 2018-19* 2019-20*  

[10:1:115,509] PROGRAM REQUIREMENTS  

[11:0:75,522] 2420 GEOLOGIC HAZARDS AND MINERAL RESOURCES CONSERVATION

[2:0:66,64] The California Department of Forestry and Fire Protection (CAL FIRE) serves and safeguards the people and protects the  

[2:1:66,74] property and resources of California.  

[3:0:66,95] CAL FIRE provides all hazard emergency - fire, medical, rescue and disaster - response to the public and provides leadership  

[3:1:66,105] in the protection of life, property and natural resources.  

[4:0:66,125] CAL FIRE safeguards the public through: engineering; research, development and adoption of regulations; fire and life safety  

[5:0:66,135] programs; fire prevention, law enforcement, and public information & education; resource protection; and emergency response.  

[6:0:66,156] CAL FIRE limits the damage caused by fires, disasters, environmental degradation, and related emergencies by employing  

[6:1:66,166] diverse yet complementary efforts including: training California's fire service professionals; public education and prevention  

[6:2:66,176] awareness; responsible stewardship of our natural resources; and natural resource and emergency management.  

[7:0:66,197] CAL FIRE's highly trained professionals and leaders cultivate mutually beneficial and cost-effective relationships with all levels  

[7:1:66,207] of government and the private sector to enhance the efficiency and effectiveness of public safety programs and mitigate  

[7:2:66,217] emergencies in the urban, rural, and wild land areas of California.  

[8:0:66,237] CAL FIRE promotes a culture of innovation, accountability, integrity, diversity, planning, workplace safety, and teamwork to  

[8:1:66,248] foster an efficient and effective organizational environment.  

[9:0:66,268] Because CAL FIRE's programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, CAL FIRE has a capital outlay program to support  

[9:1:66,278] this need. For the specifics on the CAL FIRE's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview."

[16:0:66,509] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES  

[17:0:65,527] • Increasing Fire Prevention—$224.3 million from various funds to (1) complete more fuel reduction projects through the  

[17:1:76,537] operation of prescribed fire crews and grants for forest health projects; (2) implement the recently enacted wildfire prevention  

[17:2:76,547] and recovery legislative package, which streamlines regulatory barriers for fuel reduction projects; (3) support the Board of  

[17:3:76,558] Forestry and Fire Protection in certifying the Programmatic Environmental Impact Report for the California Vegetation  

[17:4:76,568] Treatment Program; (4) enhance land use plannning and wildfire risk reduction efforts in the wildland urban interface; and  

[17:5:76,578] (5) dispose of illegal and dangerous fireworks.  

[18:0:65,594] • Expanding Firefighting Surge Capacity—$64.4 million General Fund to enhance CAL FIRE's fire protection capabilities,  

[18:1:76,604] including resources to (1) add 13 new year-round fire engines that will be located in areas of the state with the highest fire  

[18:2:76,615] risk, (2) expand heavy fire equipment operator staffing to support CAL FIRE’s bulldozer operations during emergency wildfire  

[18:3:76,625] events, (3) accelerate the replacement of fire engines and other mobile equipment to address increased wear and tear on  

[18:4:76,635] CAL FIRE’s vehicles resulting from the longer fire season, and (4) operate five additional CAL FIRE/California Conservation  

[18:5:76,645] Corps fire crews.  

[19:0:65,661] • Enhancing Aviation Resources—$127.2 million General Fund to add aircraft with increased tactical capabilities to  

[19:1:76,672] CAL FIRE’s aviation fleet to meet the challenges associated with more severe wildfire activity. Specifically, the Budget  

[19:2:76,682] includes $17.7 million General Fund for the first year of operating the large air tankers that will be transferred from the  

[19:3:76,692] U.S. Air Force. Federal legislation authorized the transfer of seven C-130 air tankers to CAL FIRE, and the Budget assumes  

[19:4:76,702] that one to two planes will be transferred each year beginning in 2019-20. The Budget also includes $109.5 million

[0:0:66,13] 2019-20 STATE BUDGET — RES   74

[2:0:129,32] 3540   Department of Forestry and Fire Protection - Continued 

[3:0:66,69] 2461 - STATE FIRE MARSHAL  

[4:0:66,89] The Office of the State Fire Marshal is responsible for the statewide protection of life and property through the development  

[4:1:66,99] and application of fire protection engineering, education and enforcement.  

[5:0:66,117] 2461010 - Office of the State Fire Marshal (OSFM) includes the following activities:  

[6:0:65,135] • Code Development and Analysis: Objectives include the development and adoption of codes relating to fire/life safety used  

[6:1:76,145] statewide by local fire and building authorities. The Code Development and Analysis Division fosters, promotes and develops  

[6:2:76,155] ways and means of protecting life and property against fire in many ways, including the adoption and implementation of  

[6:3:76,165] regulations for statewide application. This division prepares the OSFM fire and life safety regulations and building standards  

[6:4:76,175] for review and adoption into the California Code of Regulations, Titles 19 and 24. The division assists with the application of  

[6:5:76,186] state laws, regulations, and code enforcement relating by a city, county, fire departments or fire districts, and building  

[6:6:76,196] departments. This division also reviews all of California's regulations relating to fire and life safety for relevancy, necessity,  

[6:7:76,206] conflict, duplication and/or overlap.  

[7:0:65,222] • Fire and Life Safety: This program is the main entity within the department for the application of laws and regulations related  

[7:1:76,232] to fire prevention and life safety. This is achieved through code compliance inspections of new and existing buildings and  

[7:2:76,243] plan review and construction inspections of state-owned and state-occupied projects. The OSFM is responsible for fire & life  

[7:3:76,253] safety in over 45,000 state-owned and state-occupied facilities, which include: state prisons, conservation camps, community  

[7:4:76,263] correctional facilities, state mental hospitals, state developmental centers, California State University and University of  

[7:5:76,273] California campuses, and California Agricultural Districts.  

[8:0:65,289] • Fire Engineering and Investigations: This division administers licensing programs and provides services for product  

[8:1:76,300] evaluation, certifications and listings. This division also oversees the Arson and Bomb Unit, which has the responsibility to  

[8:2:76,310] dispose seized illegal fireworks and conduct fire/explosion investigations and licensing enforcement. The division is  

[8:3:76,320] responsible for 11 statewide programs, which include the Building Materials Listing; Portable Fire Extinguishers; Flame  

[8:4:76,330] Retardant Chemicals and Fabrics; Vapor Recovery; Automatic Fire Extinguishing Systems; Fire Safe Cigarettes; Licensing  

[8:5:76,340] Enforcement and Inspection; Lab Accreditation; Fireworks; and Motion Picture and Entertainment, Arson and Bomb  

[8:6:76,351] Investigation and Fireworks Disposal. This division's primary functions are licensing, product approval, fire/explosion  

[8:7:76,361] investigations and licensing enforcement.  

[9:0:65,377] • Pipeline Safety: This program ensures the safe construction, operation and maintenance of approximately 6,500 miles of  

[9:1:76,387] intrastate hazardous liquid pipelines that transport crude oil, refined products (e.g., gasoline, diesel, jet fuel, etc.) and Highly  

[9:2:76,397] Volatile Liquids (HVL) throughout California including, but not limited to, populated urban areas, ecologically sensitive areas  

[9:3:76,408] and other high consequence areas. Pipelines that fall under the Pipeline Safety Division authority are pipelines that transport  

[9:4:76,418] hazardous liquids between facilities, such as offshore platforms, production fields, refineries, storage terminals and marine  

[9:5:76,428] terminals. OSFM regulated pipelines do not include those within production fields, marine terminals or refineries.  

[10:0:65,444] • State Fire Training: This program administers the California Fire Service Training and Education System and the Fire Service  

[10:1:76,454] Training and Education Program for federal, state and local firefighters. The program oversees a California Fire Academy  

[10:2:76,465] System for over 50 training academies that represent a partnership with the fire departments, community colleges and the  

[10:3:76,475] OSFM. State Fire Training administers a professional certification system for firefighters and chief officers and receives  

[10:4:76,485] program guidance from the State Board of Fire Services.  

[11:0:66,503] 2461019 - California Underground Facilities Safe Excavation Board:  

[12:0:66,523] Also called the “Dig Safe Board,” created by the passage of Chapter 809, Statutes of 2016 (SB 661), works on issues related to  

[12:1:66,533] the State's "Call Before You Dig" law, which requires excavators and buried infrastructure owners to coordinate to prevent  

[12:2:66,544] infrastructure damage and unsafe outcomes. The Dig Safe Board is charged with coordinating the state’s safe excavation  

[12:3:66,554] outreach and education efforts, developing safe excavation standards, investigating accidents and working with partner state  

[12:4:66,564] agencies to enforce the “Call Before You Dig” law.  

[13:0:66,582] 2465 - FIRE PROTECTION  

[14:0:66,599] CAL FIRE provides fire protection to keep damages to life, property and natural resources within social, political and economic  

[14:1:66,610] constraints. The objective is to quickly and aggressively attack all fires in areas where CAL FIRE has assumed primary direct  

[14:2:66,620] protection responsibility by virtue of law, contract or agreement until the fire is under control. The level of initial attack and  

[14:3:66,630] follow-up action is relative to values threatened and control difficulty with the goal to control all unwanted fires within the first  

[14:4:66,640] burning period.  

[15:0:66,658] 2465010 - Fire Prevention:  

[16:0:66,676] This program is responsible for implementation of the Strategic Fire Plan, fire engineering, law enforcement, fire suppression  

[16:1:66,686] cost recovery and fire prevention education. This program focuses on the most effective methods, materials and procedures to  

[16:2:66,696] mitigate hazard and to enforce pertinent laws that deter and mitigate fires. More specifically, efforts focus on what needs to be  

[17:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[0:0:66,13] NATURAL RESOURCES 

[1:0:412,13] 2019-20 STATE BUDGET — RES   75 

[2:0:129,32] 3540   Department of Forestry and Fire Protection - Continued 

[3:0:66,69] done before a wildland fire starts in order to reduce the costs of fire fighting, property loss, injury to the public and fire fighters  

[3:1:66,79] and damage to the environment.  

[4:0:66,97] 2465019 - Fire Control:  

[5:0:66,107] The objective of this program is to detect, respond and suppress wildland fires in or threatening the State Responsibility Area.  

[5:1:66,117] The heart of the effort is an aggressive initial attack strategy. CAL FIRE's goal is to contain 95 percent of all wildfires to  

[5:2:66,127] 10 acres or less. This is achieved through detection, ground attack, air attack and mutual aid using fire engines, fire crews,  

[5:3:66,137] bulldozers, helicopters and fixed wing aircraft.  

[6:0:66,155] 2465028 - Cooperative Fire Protection:  

[7:0:66,173] CAL FIRE provides a full range of fire protection services to local, county, state and federal agencies throughout California  

[7:1:66,183] through the administration of over 100 cooperative government agreements. Additionally, there are six long standing  

[7:2:66,193] agreements wherein counties provide wildland fire protection on behalf of the Department.  

[8:0:66,211] 2465037 - Conservation Camps:  

[9:0:66,231] CAL FIRE, in cooperation with the Department of Corrections and Rehabilitation, Divisions of Adult Operations and Juvenile  

[9:1:66,241] Justice, operates 39 conservation camps throughout the state that house 196 fire crews. CAL FIRE also operates three Fire  

[9:2:66,252] Centers with the California Conservation Corps (CCC) throughout the state that house seven fire crews. These crews provide  

[9:3:66,262] one of the primary labor forces for firefighting, emergency response, and conservation related work projects.  

[10:0:66,280] 2465046 - Emergency Fire Suppression:  

[11:0:66,297] CAL FIRE incurs additional emergency fire suppression expenditures when budgeted initial attack forces are unable to cope  

[11:1:66,307] with a wildland fire within the initial attack period. Portions of these costs may be reimbursed in the fiscal year the expense was  

[11:2:66,318] incurred or in a future fiscal year via Fire Management Assistance Grants provided by the Federal Emergency Management  

[11:3:66,328] Agency (FEMA) or through cost shares provided by other government entities. In addition, CAL FIRE may recover portions of  

[11:4:66,338] this cost through civil cost recovery actions authorized by state statutes.  

[12:0:66,356] 2470 - RESOURCE MANAGEMENT  

[13:0:66,373] California's state and private forest, range, watershed lands and urban forests provide multiple human, climate and  

[13:1:66,384] environmental benefits. The objective of this program is to maintain and enhance those benefits and to minimize damage to  

[13:2:66,394] these resources from natural catastrophes and human development. Objectives are met by regulation of timber harvesting,  

[13:3:66,404] coordination of climate related activities for the forest sector, technical assistance to non-industrial landowners, operation of  

[13:4:66,414] state demonstration forests, operation of forest nurseries, vegetation management projects and administration of federal  

[13:5:66,424] forestry assistance programs.  

[14:0:66,442] 2470010 - Resources Protection and Improvement:  

[15:0:66,452] The objective of this program is to improve forest lands and urban forests. Activities include the detection, evaluation and  

[15:1:66,463] control of forest pests; growing and selling tree seedlings for reforestation, carbon sequestration, and soil erosion control;  

[15:2:66,473] maintenance of a native conifer seed bank; advice and assistance to non-industrial forest landowners on management of  

[15:3:66,483] forests and improved harvesting and conservation practices; research on and demonstration of sustainable forestry in state  

[15:4:66,493] forests; implementation of the California Forest Improvement Act of 1978; technical assistance and grants to foster the  

[15:5:66,503] environmental, social, and economic benefits of urban forests consistent with the California Urban Forestry Act of 1978; and  

[15:6:66,514] purchase and monitoring of working forest conservation easements per the California Forest Legacy Act of 2008. In the area of  

[15:7:66,524] vegetation management, assistance is provided to public and private landowners to achieve land use objectives by reducing  

[15:8:66,534] damage from wildland fires, increase wildlife habitat, increase productivity of forest and rangelands, improve water yields and  

[15:9:66,544] air quality, reduce carbon emissions from wildland fires, increase firefighter safety, and maintain desirable ecosystems.  

[15:10:66,554] CAL FIRE cooperates with federal, state and local agencies, local organizations, and private property owners to develop and  

[15:11:66,565] achieve land use objectives. Activities include removal, rearrangement, conversion, or improvement of vegetation using various  

[15:12:66,575] treatment measures such as prescribed fire and mechanical, manual, and biological methods. The Environmental Protection  

[15:13:66,585] Program provides in-house expertise and oversight of the Department's compliance with the California Environmental Quality  

[15:14:66,595] Act, the National Environmental Policy Act and other environmental laws and regulations applicable to departmental actions.  

[16:0:66,613] 2470019 - Forest Practice Regulations:  

[17:0:66,631] The objective of this program is to maximize sustained production of high-quality timber products while protecting soil, water,  

[17:1:66,641] wildlife, recreation and other values associated with forest land. Forest Practice activities include the review and permitting of  

[17:2:66,651] commercial timber harvesting operations on nonfederal timberlands, enforcing state forest practice laws and regulations, and  

[17:3:66,661] monitoring compliance and the effects of timber operations on forest productivity, water quality, watersheds, soils, botanical  

[17:4:66,671] resources, wildlife, fisheries, greenhouse gases and other environmentally sensitive resources. The Program is also  

[17:5:66,682] responsible for issuing licenses to timber operators (LTOs), providing LTO training and education, and assisting the Board of  

[17:6:66,692] Equalization-Timber Tax Division in their administration of taxes on timber and timberland. Forest Practice Program staff also  

[17:7:66,702] assist the Board of Forestry and Fire Protection, and facilitate and participate in monitoring the effectiveness of the Board's  

[17:8:66,712] forest practice regulations.  

[18:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[3:0:66,69] 2470028 - Forest Resource Inventory and Assessment:  

[4:0:66,86] The objective of this program is to perform a periodic assessment of California's forest and rangeland resources. Activities  

[4:1:66,97] include maintaining and distributing mission-critical datasets for fire planning, emergency incident support and meeting certain  

[4:2:66,107] regulatory requirements. In addition, the program provides extensive technical and public information for statewide fire threat,  

[4:3:66,117] fire hazard, watersheds, socio-economic conditions, environmental indicators and urban forestry.  

[4:4:66,135] 2470037 - Professional Foresters Registration Program:  

[5:0:66,152] The Office of Professional Foresters Registration is a statutorily authorized program within the State Board of Forestry and Fire  

[5:1:66,163] Protection. The program is responsible for the licensing and regulation of all persons who practice forestry or rangeland  

[5:2:66,173] management on non-federal, state and private lands in California pursuant to the Professional Foresters Law of 1972.  

[5:3:66,190] 2475 - BOARD OF FORESTRY AND FIRE PROTECTION  

[6:0:66,211] The State Board of Forestry and Fire Protection (Board) is a Governor-appointed body within CAL FIRE. The Board is  

[6:1:66,221] responsible for developing the general forest policy of the state, for determining the guidance policies of CAL FIRE, and for  

[6:2:66,231] representing the state's interest in Federal land in California. Together, the Board and CAL FIRE work to carry out the California  

[6:3:66,241] Legislature's mandate to protect and enhance the State's unique forest and wildland resources. The Board's statutory  

[6:4:66,252] responsibilities are:  

[7:0:65,269] • Establish and administer forest and rangeland policy for the State of California;  

[7:1:65,286] • Protect and represent the state's interest in all forestry and rangeland matters;  

[8:0:65,302] • Provide direction and guidance to the Director and CAL FIRE on fire protection and resource management;  

[8:1:65,318] • Deliver a comprehensive regulatory program for forestry and fire protection;  

[8:2:65,334] • Conduct its duties to inform and respond to the people of California.  

[8:3:66,352] 2480 - DEPARTMENT OF JUSTICE LEGAL SERVICES  

[9:0:66,370] The objective of the Department of Justice Legal Services program is to account for the cost of legal services provided by the  

[9:1:66,380] Department of Justice to the California Department of Forestry and Fire Protection.  

[9:2:66,397] 9900100 - ADMINISTRATION  

[10:0:66,415] Departmental administration provides executive leadership, policy direction and a variety of program support services  

[10:1:66,425] necessary for the successful completion of CAL FIRE's mission. CAL FIRE headquarters provides leadership through the  

[10:2:66,436] executive office and through central services in accounting, budgeting, business services, legal, human resources, information  

[10:3:66,446] technology, program accountability and program and systems analysis. CAL FIRE field units provide localized general support  

[10:4:66,456] services throughout in the state.

[1:0:215,43] 3560   State Lands Commission  

[2:0:66,64] The California State Lands Commission provides the people of California with effective stewardship of the lands, waterways,  

[2:1:66,74] and resources entrusted to its care through preservation, restoration, enhancement, responsible economic development, and  

[2:2:66,84] the promotion of public access. Diligent execution of the Commission's responsibilities since its inception in 1938 has resulted  

[2:3:66,95] in the generation of over $11.6 billion in revenues, while protecting and enhancing the public's ability to enjoy those lands and  

[2:4:66,105] resources.  

[3:0:66,123] Consisting of the Lieutenant Governor, the State Controller, and the Governor's Director of Finance, the California State Lands  

[3:1:66,133] Commission serves as a trustee for the people of the state, managing California's sovereign public trust lands and resources,  

[3:2:66,143] which the state received upon admission into the Union in 1850. It also manages other lands subsequently conveyed to the  

[3:3:66,153] state by the federal government (commonly known as "school" lands) and oversees the management of public trust lands  

[3:4:66,163] legislatively granted in trust to over 70 local jurisdictions. These grants encourage development and use of the state's  

[3:5:66,174] tidelands, consistent with the public trust doctrine, and typically require grantees to reinvest revenues produced from the  

[3:6:66,184] granted lands back into the trust.  

[4:0:66,201] Public trust or "sovereign" lands include the beds of all natural and navigable waterways, including non-tidal rivers, streams and  

[4:1:66,212] lakes, and tide and submerged lands within tidal rivers, sloughs, bays, and the Pacific Ocean extending from the mean high  

[4:2:66,222] tide line seaward to the three-mile offshore limit and totaling over four and one-half million acres. In addition to sovereign lands,  

[4:3:66,232] the Commission manages "school" lands, which were granted to California by the federal government under the Act of March  

[4:4:66,242] 3, 1853 (10 Stat. 244) for the purpose of supporting public education in California. School lands include the 16th and 36th  

[4:5:66,252] sections of each township (with the exception of lands already reserved for public use or previously taken by private claims)  

[4:6:66,263] and lands known to be mineral in character. Of the five and one-half million acres of school lands originally granted to the state,  

[4:7:66,273] only about 458,843 acres remain in state ownership, and these are mostly concentrated in the California desert. The  

[4:8:66,283] Commission also retains a reserved mineral interest in approximately 790,000 acres of sold school lands.

[12:0:66,508] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[12:1:66,526] 2560 - MINERAL RESOURCES MANAGEMENT  

[13:0:66,547] The California State Lands Commission oversees efficient and safe development of mineral resources that are located on state  

[13:1:66,557] lands. The State also has a beneficial interest in the Long Beach tidelands oils fields, and as such, the Commission participates  

[13:2:66,567] in the safety and fiscal oversight of these fields. The program objectives are to: manage the responsible extraction of oil, gas,  

[13:3:66,577] geothermal resources and other minerals; collect revenues generated consistent with the best interests of the state; and ensure  

[13:4:66,587] that public safety and environmental protection are maintained at the highest possible standards in the development of these  

[13:5:66,598] resources through engineering review, marine facility inspections, and safety audits. The Commission also adopts regulations  

[13:6:66,608] for the prevention of oil spills, the safe operation of offshore facilities and program and financial audits to ensure the state  

[13:7:66,618] receives fair value for the development of its resources.  

[13:8:66,636] 2565 - LAND MANAGEMENT  

[14:0:66,656] The California State Lands Commission manages all ungranted state sovereign lands consistent with the common law public  

[14:1:66,666] trust doctrine and prudent land use and management practices. The Commission issues leases and permits for the use and  

[14:2:66,677] occupation of public trust lands under its jurisdiction based upon environmental, economic, health, safety and public benefit  

[14:3:66,687] considerations. The program also manages all state school lands to ensure the greatest economic return to the California   State  

[14:4:66,697] Teachers' Retirement System. The Commission also oversees the administration of public trust lands legislatively granted to  

[14:5:66,707] local jurisdictions to manage on behalf of the State, including the major ports in the State.  

[15:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[3:0:66,69] 2570 - MARINE ENVIRONMENTAL PROTECTION DIVISION  

[4:0:66,89] The California   State Lands Commission adopts rules, regulations, and guidelines for marine oil terminals within California,  

[4:1:66,99] providing the best achievable protection of the public health and safety and the environment. The Commission's Marine  

[4:2:66,109] Environmental Protection Program monitors the transfer of oil at marine terminals, inspects all marine facilities, and reviews  

[4:3:66,120] and approves all marine oil terminal operations manuals and training and certification programs. The Program also administers  

[4:4:66,130] Chapter 31F of the California Building Code to ensure that marine oil terminals are fit for purpose. In addition, the Marine  

[4:5:66,140] Environmental Protection Program implements the state's Marine Invasive Species Program to prevent the introduction of non-

[4:6:66,150] indigenous species into California waters through vessel mediated vectors. The program funds and conducts scientific  

[4:7:66,160] research, develops rules and regulations to implement the Marine Invasive Species Act, and physically inspects oceangoing  

[4:8:66,171] vessels to assess compliance with vessel-reported nonindigenous species management actions.  

[4:9:66,188] 9900100 - EXECUTIVE AND ADMINISTRATION  

[5:0:66,206] The Executive and Administration program provides management, policy direction and administrative support to the line  

[5:1:66,216] programs of the Commission.

[1:0:197,43] 3600   Department of Fish and Wildlife  

[2:0:66,64] The mission of the Department of Fish and Wildlife is to manage California's diverse fish, wildlife, and plant resources, and the  

[2:1:66,74] habitats upon which they depend, for their ecological values and for their use and enjoyment by the public.  

[3:0:66,95] This includes habitat protection and maintenance in a sufficient amount and quality to ensure the survival of all species and  

[3:1:66,105] natural communities. The Department is also responsible for the diversified use of fish and wildlife including recreational,  

[3:2:66,115] commercial, scientific, and educational uses.  

[4:0:66,135] Because the Department of Fish and Wildlife programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the Department has a capital  

[4:1:66,146] outlay program to support this need. For the specifics on the Department's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure  

[4:2:66,156] Overview."

[3:0:66,88] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES  

[3:1:65,106] • Wildfire Prevention and Recovery Legislative Package (SB 901)—The Budget includes $3.5 million ($1.5 million General  

[3:2:76,116] Fund and $2 million Timber Regulation and Forest Restoration Fund) in 2019-20, and ongoing funding, for the Department to  

[3:3:76,127] address workload associated with recently chaptered wildfire prevention and recovery legislation including increased  

[3:4:76,137] environmental review and permitting of projects that improve forest health and vegetation management activities to ensure  

[3:5:76,147] compliance with the Timber Harvest Plan Exemptions, Lake and Streambed Alteration Program, California Environmental  

[3:6:76,157] Quality Act, and California Endangered Species Act.

[2:0:162,32] 3600   Department of Fish and Wildlife - Continued 

[3:0:66,88] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[3:1:66,106] 2590 - BIODIVERSITY CONSERVATION PROGRAM  

[4:0:66,127] This program encourages the preservation, conservation, maintenance, and restoration of wildlife resources, including the  

[4:1:66,137] Ecosystem Restoration Program, under the jurisdiction and influence of the state. Activities involve the conservation, protection  

[4:2:66,147] and management of fish, wildlife, native plants, and habitat to ensure maintenance of biologically sustainable populations of  

[4:3:66,157] those species.  

[5:0:66,175] 2595 - HUNTING, FISHING, AND PUBLIC USE PROGRAM  

[6:0:66,193] This program facilitates diverse and sustainable hunting, fishing (recreational and commercial), trapping, and other public uses  

[6:1:66,203] and associated economic benefits to the state by conserving and managing game species. Activities include collection and  

[6:2:66,213] assessment of information on the distribution and abundance of game fish and wildlife to determine appropriate regulations  

[6:3:66,223] (bag limits, gear restrictions, etc.) and to monitor the effects of those regulations.  

[6:4:66,241] 2600 - MANAGEMENT OF DEPARTMENT LANDS AND FACILITIES PROGRAM  

[6:5:66,259] This program manages Department-owned or leased lands and facilities, including hatcheries, wildlife areas, ecological  

[6:6:66,269] reserves, fish and wildlife laboratories, and public access areas, to contribute to the conservation, protection, and management  

[6:7:66,279] of fish and wildlife.  

[6:8:66,297] 2605 - ENFORCEMENT  

[7:0:66,314] This program serves the public through law enforcement, public safety and hunter education. Law enforcement promotes  

[7:1:66,325] compliance with laws and regulations protecting fish and wildlife resources; investigates habitat destruction, pollution incidents  

[7:2:66,335] and illegal commercialization of wildlife. Wardens also serve the public through general law enforcement, mutual aid and  

[7:3:66,345] homeland security.  

[8:0:66,363] 2610 - COMMUNICATIONS, EDUCATION, AND OUTREACH PROGRAM  

[9:0:66,380] This program serves the public through resource conservation education and use activities in the classroom and on public and  

[9:1:66,391] private lands, community and stakeholder outreach, and the delivery of information and data using a variety of methods  

[9:2:66,401] including publications, presentations, web applications and media relations.  

[9:3:66,418] 2615 - SPILL PREVENTION AND RESPONSE PROGRAM  

[10:0:66,436] This program prevents damage, minimizes environmental impacts, restores, and rehabilitates California's fish and wildlife  

[10:1:66,446] populations and their habitats from the harmful effects of oil and other deleterious material spills in marine waters and inland  

[10:2:66,457] habitats.  

[10:3:66,474] 2620 - FISH AND GAME COMMISSION  

[11:0:66,492] The California Fish and Game Commission ensures the long term sustainability of California's fish and wildlife resources by  

[11:1:66,502] guiding the ongoing scientific evaluation and assessment of California's fish and wildlife resources; setting California's fish and  

[11:2:66,512] wildlife resource management policies and ensuring these are implemented by the Department of Fish and Wildlife;  

[11:3:66,523] establishing appropriate fish and wildlife resource management rules and regulations; and building active fish and wildlife  

[11:4:66,533] resource management partnerships with individual landowners, the public and interest groups, and federal, state and local  

[11:5:66,543] resource management agencies.  

[12:0:66,580] DETAILED EXPENDITURES BY PROGRAM  †

[12:0:66,563] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW  

[13:0:66,581] The Department of Fish and Wildlife manages 742 properties statewide, comprising more than 1 million acres (approximately  

[13:1:66,592] 679,000 acres of state-owned land and 484,000 acres owned by other entities, but managed by the Department. Several state  

[13:2:66,602] agencies purchase land for the purpose of habitat or wildlife protection, and management responsibilities for these properties  

[13:3:66,612] are often transferred to the Department; therefore, the amount of land under Department control continues to increase. The  

[13:4:66,622] properties managed by the Department include: 111 wildlife areas, 136 ecological reserves, 138 public access areas, and 21  

[13:5:66,632] fish hatcheries.

[7:1:66,371] 2710 - WILDLIFE CONSERVATION BOARD  

[8:0:66,391] The Wildlife Conservation Board's three main functions are (1) land acquisition, (2) habitat protection, restoration, and  

[8:1:66,402] enhancement, including conservation of inland wetlands, riparian habitat, oak woodlands and protection of rangeland, grazing  

[8:2:66,412] land and grasslands, and (3) development of wildlife-oriented public access and recreational areas.

[2:1:66,74] Committee, and the Speaker of the Assembly, was created by voter initiative in 1972 and was made permanent by the  

[3:0:66,84] California Coastal Act of 1976 (Coastal Act). The Coastal Act calls for the protection and enhancement of public access and  

[3:1:66,95] recreation, marine resources, environmentally sensitive habitat areas, marine water quality, agriculture, and scenic resources,  

[3:2:66,105] and makes provisions for coastal-dependent industrial and energy development. New development in the coastal zone requires  

[3:3:66,115] a coastal permit either from local government or the Commission. Local governments are required to prepare a local coastal  

[3:4:66,125] program (LCP) for the coastal zone portion of their jurisdiction. After an LCP has been reviewed and approved by the  

[3:5:66,135] Commission as being consistent with the Coastal Act, the Commission's regulatory authority over most types of new  

[4:0:66,146] development is delegated to the local government, subject to limited appeals to the Commission. The Commission also is  

[4:1:66,156] designated the principal state coastal management agency for the purpose of administering the federal Coastal Zone  

[5:0:66,166] Management Act in California and has exclusive regulatory authority over federal activities such as permits, leases, federal  

[5:1:66,176] development projects, and other federal actions that could affect coastal zone resources and that would not otherwise be  

[5:2:66,186] subject to state control.

[7:0:66,447] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[7:1:66,465] 2730 - COASTAL MANAGEMENT PROGRAM  

[8:0:66,485] The objectives of the Coastal Management Program are to implement coastal resources conservation through planning and  

[8:1:66,495] regulation. Activities include:  

[9:0:65,513] • Reviewing and approval of local coastal programs (LCPs), port master plans, university long-range development plans, and  

[9:1:76,523] any amendments to such plans, for consistency with the Coastal Act.  

[10:0:65,540] • Reviewing coastal development permit applications for new development in areas without a certified LCP, areas of  

[10:1:76,550] permanently retained jurisdiction (e.g., tidelands, submerged lands, and public trust lands) and limited categories of local  

[10:2:76,560] coastal development permit actions that can be appealed to the Commission.  

[10:3:65,576] • Monitoring and enforcement of coastal development permits.  

[10:4:65,592] • Reviewing federal activities for consistency with the Coastal Act.  

[10:5:65,609] • Protecting and expanding opportunities for public coastal access and recreation.  

[10:6:65,625] • Implementing a coastal water quality protection program.  

[11:0:65,641] • Providing technical information and assistance to support effective coastal management.  

[11:1:65,657] • Implementing a coastal and ocean resource public education program.  

[11:2:66,675] 2735 - COASTAL ENERGY PROGRAM  

[12:0:66,695] The Coastal Energy Program addresses coastal energy issues including, but not limited to, offshore oil and gas development,  

[12:1:66,705] electricity generating power plant expansion and development, and siting and development of liquefied natural gas facilities.  

[12:2:66,716] (Note: Program 2735 will be consolidated into Program 2730 beginning Fiscal Year 2019-20.)

[1:0:210,43] 3760   State Coastal Conservancy  

[2:0:66,64] The State Coastal Conservancy develops and implements projects to protect, restore and enhance natural, recreational, and  

[2:1:66,74] economic resources along California's coast, coastal watersheds, the ocean, and within the San Francisco Bay Area. The  

[2:2:66,84] Coastal Conservancy supports implementation of the San Francisco Bay Area Conservancy Program and the Santa Ana River  

[2:3:66,95] Conservancy Program. Through technical assistance, direct funding and grants to local agencies and nonprofit organizations,  

[2:4:66,105] the Coastal Conservancy supports projects that:  

[3:0:65,123] • Protect and enhance the natural environment, agricultural lands, scenic lands and urban waterfronts along the Coast and  

[3:1:76,133] around San Francisco Bay.  

[4:0:65,149] • Increase public access to the coast and San Francisco Bay  

[4:1:65,165] • Build trails and increase outdoor recreational opportunities.  

[4:2:65,181] • Enhance coastal wetlands and other important habitat areas.  

[5:0:65,198] • Prepare for and reduce the unavoidable impacts of climate change on the coastal region and San Francisco Bay Area.  

[5:1:65,214] • Support education programs about coastal resources for kindergarten through grade 12.

[2:0:175,32] 3760   State Coastal Conservancy - Continued 

[3:0:66,69] 2790 - COASTAL CONSERVANCY PROGRAM  

[4:0:66,89] The Coastal Conservancy seeks to achieve the following goals:  

[4:1:65,107] • Protect and enhance habitats, wetlands, scenic open space, working lands and urban waterfronts along the California coast  

[4:2:76,117] and around San Francisco Bay.  

[5:0:65,133] • Increase public access to the coast and outdoor recreational opportunities by creating new parks, trails and recreational  

[5:1:76,143] amenities.  

[6:0:65,160] • Prepare for and reduce the unavoidable impacts of climate change on the coastal region and San Francisco Bay Area.  

[6:1:65,176] • Support education programs about coastal resources for kindergarten through grade 12.  

[6:2:65,192] • Facilitate improvement of existing, and the development of new, lower cost accommodations within one and one-half miles of  

[6:3:76,202] the coast.  

[6:4:66,220] 2805 - STATE COASTAL CONSERVANCY LOCAL ASSISTANCE PROGRAM  

[7:0:66,240] The State Coastal Conservancy provides grants and technical assistance to local governments, nonprofits and other project  

[7:1:66,250] partners for the acquisition of land and easements, the development of public facilities, and the restoration of habitat. The State  

[7:2:66,261] Coastal Conservancy helps to implement the State's federally approved Coastal Management Program and supports programs  

[7:3:66,271] that increase public access to the coast. The Conservancy has participated in the protection of more than 390,000 acres of  

[7:4:66,281] coastal lands through acquisition of fee title and conservation easements. The Conservancy has helped restore more than  

[7:5:66,291] 33,000 acres of habitat.  

[8:0:66,312] Through its projects, the Conservancy has established approximately 160 new coastal accessways and built more than 168  

[8:1:66,322] miles of new trails.  

[9:0:66,342] State Coastal Conservancy funds are primarily expended for purposes of local assistance but may also be expended for capital  

[9:1:66,352] outlay purposes, see Program 2820.  

[9:2:66,370] 2820 - STATE COASTAL CONSERVANCY CAPITAL OUTLAY PROGRAM  

[10:0:66,391] The State Coastal Conservancy provides grants and technical assistance to state agencies for the acquisition of land and  

[10:1:66,401] easements, the development of public facilities, and the restoration of habitat. The State Coastal Conservancy helps to  

[10:2:66,411] implement the State's federally approved Coastal Management Program. The Conservancy has participated in the protection of  

[10:3:66,421] more than 390,000 acres of coastal lands through acquisition of fee title and conservation easements. The Conservancy has  

[10:4:66,431] helped restore more than 33,000 acres of habitat.

[1:0:183,43] 3790   Department of Parks and Recreation  

[2:0:66,64] The mission of the California Department of Parks and Recreation (Parks) is to provide for the health, inspiration, and  

[3:0:66,74] education of the people of California by helping to preserve the state's extraordinary biological diversity, protecting its most  

[3:1:66,84] valued natural, cultural and historical resources, and creating opportunities for high-quality outdoor recreation for current and  

[3:2:66,95] future generations to enjoy. With increased urbanization, the establishment of park units and recreation areas accessible to the  

[3:3:66,105] major population centers of the state has become particularly important. Specific activities include: stewardship of natural  

[3:4:66,115] resources, historic, cultural and archeological sites, and artifacts and structures; provision of interpretive services for park  

[3:5:66,125] visitors; construction and maintenance of campsites, trails, visitor centers, museums, and infrastructure such as roads and  

[3:6:66,135] water systems; and creation of recreational opportunities such as hiking, bicycling, fishing, swimming, horseback riding,  

[3:7:66,146] jogging, camping, picnicking, and off-highway vehicle recreation. In addition, the Division of Boating and Waterways funds,  

[3:8:66,156] plans, and develops boating facilities on waterways throughout California and ensures safe boating for the public by providing  

[3:9:66,166] financial aid and training to local law enforcement agencies.  

[4:0:66,186] Because Parks' programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, Parks has a capital outlay program to support this need.  

[4:1:66,197] For the specifics on Parks' capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview."

[0:0:66,13] NATURAL RESOURCES   2019-20 STATE BUDGET — RES   181 

[1:0:177,43] 3810   Santa Monica Mountains Conservancy  

[1:1:66,64] The Santa Monica Mountains Conservancy acquires, restores, and consolidates lands in the Santa Monica Mountains, Rim of  

[1:2:66,74] the Valley Trail Corridor, Upper Los Angeles River Watershed, and watersheds of Santa Monica Bay for park, recreation, and  

[1:3:66,84] conservation purposes. The Conservancy may:  

[1:4:65,102] • Lease, rent, sell, transfer, or exchange lands for park purposes.  

[2:0:65,118] • Award grants or interest free loans to state and local agencies to purchase or restore park, recreation, conservation, or  

[2:1:76,129] buffer-zone areas to ensure that the character and intensity of development on these lands is generally compatible with and  

[2:2:76,139] does not adversely impact the environment.  

[3:0:65,155] • Implement programs to improve access from the inner city areas surrounding the Conservancy, thereby providing  

[3:1:76,165] recreational opportunities for all residents.  

[3:2:65,181] • Manage the Rim of the Valley Trail Corridor recreational trail.  

[4:0:65,198] • Plans for restoration, recreation, and conservation projects for the Los Angeles River and Upper Los Angeles River  

[4:1:76,208] Watershed.
