[1:0:104,43] 3820   San Francisco Bay Conservation and Development Commission  

[2:0:66,64] The San Francisco Bay Conservation and Development Commission is a state planning and regulatory agency with regional  

[2:1:66,74] authority. Its mission is to protect and enhance San Francisco Bay and to encourage the Bay's responsible and productive use  

[2:2:66,84] for this and future generations. The Commission authored and maintains the San Francisco Bay Plan and relies on it, the  

[3:0:66,95] McAteer-Petris Act, and other regulatory authority to maximize public access to the Bay and minimize Bay fill. The Commission  

[3:1:66,105] issues permits for filling, dredging, and development projects within the Bay, along the Bay shoreline, and within salt ponds and  

[3:2:66,115] certain managed wetlands adjacent to the Bay. The Commission also implements the Suisun Marsh Preservation Act of 1977.  

[3:3:66,125] By state statute, the Commission develops and implements the federal Coastal Zone Management Act's program for the Bay  

[3:4:66,135] and exercises authority over federal activities otherwise not subject to state control. The Commission leads the ongoing multi-

[3:5:66,146] agency regional effort to address the impacts of sea level rise and climate change on the Bay and its environs. Funding for  

[3:6:66,156] these efforts to address climate change is derived mainly from federal grants and other agreements, contracts, and  

[3:7:66,166] reimbursements.  

[4:0:66,196] 3-YEAR EXPENDITURES AND POSITIONS

[1:0:195,43] 3830   San Joaquin River Conservancy  

[1:1:66,64] The mission of the San Joaquin River Conservancy is to acquire, preserve, manage, and promote access to lands within the  

[1:2:66,74] flood plain on both sides of the San Joaquin River from Friant Dam to Highway 99.  

[2:0:66,95] Specific activities are to:  

[2:1:65,112] • Implement the San Joaquin River Parkway Master Plan, a 22-mile regional greenspace and wildlife corridor along both sides  

[2:2:76,123] of the river extending from Friant Dam to Highway 99, with an interconnected trail system and recreational and educational  

[2:3:76,133] features.  

[3:0:65,149] • Acquire approximately 5,900 acres from willing sellers.  

[4:0:65,165] • Operate and manage lands for public enjoyment consistent with the protection of natural resources.  

[4:1:65,181] • Protect, enhance, and restore riparian and riverine habitat and ecological diversity.  

[4:2:65,198] • Provide for multi-benefit water quality, water supply, ecosystem and watershed protection and restoration.  

[4:3:65,214] • Facilitate the development of the parkway, garner public support, and secure its future.

[1:0:210,43] 3835   Baldwin Hills Conservancy  

[1:1:66,64] The Baldwin Hills Conservancy was established in 2000 to acquire open space and manage public lands within the Baldwin  

[1:2:66,74] Hills area and to provide recreation, restoration, and protection of wildlife habitat for the public's enjoyment and educational  

[1:3:66,84] experience.  

[1:4:66,105] Specific agency responsibilities are to:  

[1:5:65,123] • Approve the Baldwin Hills Park Master Plan.  

[2:0:65,139] • Undertake site improvement projects, regulate public access, construct new facilities as needed for outdoor recreation,  

[2:1:76,149] nature appreciation and natural resource protection.  

[3:0:65,165] • Prioritize and implement acquisition of additional recreational and open space land within the Baldwin Hills area.  

[3:1:65,181] • Provide recreational, open space, wildlife habitat restoration and protection of lands for educational use within the area.  

[3:2:65,198] • Conduct the necessary planning activities for the area.  

[4:0:65,214] • Approve conservancy funded projects that advance the policies and priorities set forth by the enabling statute.  

[4:1:65,230] • Develop and coordinate a program of resource stewardship for optimum recreational and natural resource value based on  

[4:2:76,240] the needs of the surrounding community.  

[5:0:65,256] • Provide for the public's enjoyment, and enhance recreational and educational experiences on public lands in a manner  

[5:1:76,267] consistent with the protection of lands and resources in the area.

[1:0:203,43] 3840   Delta Protection Commission  

[2:0:66,64] The mission of the Delta Protection Commission is to protect, maintain, enhance, and enrich the overall quality of the Delta  

[2:1:66,74] environment and economy, with a focus on agriculture, recreation, and natural resources,   while remaining   mindful of the  

[2:2:66,84] importance of the Delta to all Californians. The Commission ensures orderly, balanced conservation and development of Delta  

[2:3:66,95] land resources and provides a forum for Delta residents to engage in decisions regarding actions to recognize and enhance the  

[2:4:66,105] unique cultural, recreational, and agricultural resources of the Delta. The Commission identifies and makes recommendations  

[2:5:66,115] on methods of preserving the Delta as an evolving place and promotes Delta legacy communities, regional economic  

[2:6:66,125] sustainability, and emergency response preparedness.

[1:0:202,43] 3845   San Diego River Conservancy  

[2:0:66,64] The mission of the San Diego River Conservancy is to achieve the goals of the San Diego River Conservancy Act by  

[2:1:66,74] implementing five   six major programs: Land Conservation, Recreation and Education, Preserve Natural/Cultural Resources,  

[2:2:66,84] Water Quality and Natural Flood Conveyance, Organizational Capacity and Reach, and the San Diego Rivers Watershed  

[2:3:66,95] Consortium. This mission will be accomplished in part by building, with our partners, a San Diego River Park and hiking trail  

[2:4:66,105] stretching from the headwaters in Julian to the Pacific Ocean and creating advisory panels to assist in restoring and enhancing  

[2:5:66,115] three nearby rivers from their headlands to the Pacific Ocean - the Otay River, Sweetwater River and Tijuana River. These  

[2:6:66,125] programs foster collaboration among governments and nonprofit organizations, and leverage grant funding to achieve an array  

[2:7:66,135] of conservation objectives.

[5:0:66,140] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[5:1:66,159] 3220 - SIERRA NEVADA CONSERVANCY PROGRAM  

[5:2:66,179] Established pursuant to Chapter 726, Statutes of 2004, the Conservancy is authorized to:  

[5:3:65,197] • Provide increased opportunities for tourism and recreation.  

[5:4:65,213] • Protect, conserve, and restore the region's physical, cultural, archaeological, historical, and living resources.  

[5:5:65,229] • Aid in the preservation of working landscapes.  

[5:6:65,245] • Reduce the risk of natural disasters, such as wildfires.  

[5:7:65,262] • Protect and improve water and air quality.  

[5:8:65,278] • Assist the regional economy through the operation of the Conservancy's program.  

[5:9:65,294] • Undertake efforts to enhance public use and enjoyment of lands owned by the public.  

[5:10:66,312] In support of these programs, the Conservancy facilitates collaborative planning efforts with local and regional governments,  

[5:11:66,322] community groups and interested parties, provides technical assistance, and initiates and funds projects to identify and fill  

[5:12:66,332] critical needs in the Sierra Nevada region.

[1:0:196,43] 3860   Department of Water Resources  

[2:0:66,64] The Department of Water Resources protects, conserves, develops, and manages California's water. The Department  

[3:0:66,74] evaluates existing water resources, forecasts future water needs and explores future potential solutions to meet ever-growing  

[3:1:66,84] needs for personal use, irrigation, industry, recreation, power generation, and fish and wildlife. The Department also works to  

[3:2:66,95] prevent and minimize flood damage, oversee the safety of dams, and educate the public about the importance of water and its  

[3:3:66,105] efficient use.  

[4:0:66,125] Because the Department's programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the Department has a capital outlay program to  

[4:1:66,135] support this need. For the specifics on the Department's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview."

[5:0:66,337] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[6:0:66,355] 3230 - CONTINUING FORMULATION OF THE CALIFORNIA WATER PLAN  

[7:0:66,376] The California Water Plan is the state's strategic plan for the efficient use, management and development of the state's water  

[7:1:66,386] resources. The Plan is updated every five years and provides a framework for water managers, legislators, and the public to  

[7:2:66,396] consider options and make decisions regarding California's water future. The Plan evaluates current and future water  

[7:3:66,406] conditions, challenges and opportunities. It presents basic data and information on California's water resources including water  

[7:4:66,416] supply evaluations and assessments of existing and future agricultural, urban, and environmental water uses. The Plan  

[7:5:66,427] identifies and evaluates multiple future scenarios and over 30 resource management strategies such as conservation,  

[7:6:66,437] recycling, desalination, transfers, storage, conveyance, water quality, watershed management, ecosystem restoration,  

[7:7:66,447] groundwater, and urban land use management to help meet future demands in light of uncertainties and unexpected  

[7:8:66,457] catastrophic events. This program also identifies ways for the state to: (1) help local agencies and governments prepare  

[7:9:66,467] integrated regional water management plans on a watershed basis and diversify their regional water portfolios to ensure  

[7:10:66,478] sustainable water uses, reliable water supplies, better water quality, environmental stewardship, efficient urban development,  

[7:11:66,488] protection of agriculture, and a strong economy, (2) help cities, counties and local agencies prepare a Water Element for their  

[7:12:66,498] General Plans, Urban Water Management Plans and Agricultural Water Management Plans, and (3) help local agencies and  

[7:13:66,508] tribal governments improve coordination between water and land use planning.  

[8:0:66,526] 3240 - IMPLEMENTATION OF THE STATE WATER RESOURCES DEVELOPMENT SYSTEM  

[9:0:66,544] The State Water Project is a water storage and delivery system that consists of 34 storage facilities (reservoirs and lakes), 21  

[9:1:66,554] pumping plants, 4 pumping-generating plants, 5 hydroelectric power plants, and over 700 miles of conveyance (canals,  

[9:2:66,564] pipelines, and tunnels). The Project provides water to over 25 million Californians and 750,000 acres of irrigated farmland.  

[10:0:66,582] The Department plans, designs, constructs, operates, maintains, and manages State Water Project facilities which provide  

[10:1:66,592] water to facilities located from Plumas County to Riverside County.  

[11:0:66,610] The California WaterFix Program is charged with improving the Delta ecosystem and ensuring water supply reliability in a safe,  

[11:1:66,620] timely, and cost-effective manner. This includes implementation of the California WaterFix, project specific environmental  

[11:2:66,630] commitments, and compliance with the Environmental Impact Report and Environmental Impact Statement and other state and  

[11:3:66,640] federal regulations and permits.  

[12:0:66,658] 3245 - PUBLIC SAFETY AND PREVENTION OF DAMAGE  

[13:0:66,676] This program supports the California Water Action Plan by protecting life and property from damage by floods, ensures proper  

[13:1:66,686] construction and maintenance of jurisdictional dams and levees, and provides loans for the construction. Activities include  

[13:2:66,696] assessing the state and regional investment needs to reduce risk, preventive floodplain management to discourage unwise use  

[13:3:66,706] of areas subject to flooding, protection of floodplains, issuance of flood warnings, operation of flood control facilities,  

[14:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[0:0:66,13] NATURAL RESOURCES 

[1:0:408,13] 2019-20 STATE BUDGET — RES   227 

[2:0:161,32] 3860   Department of Water Resources - Continued 

[3:0:66,69] coordination and supervision of flood fight activities, and annual levee and flood channel maintenance and inspection in  

[3:1:66,79] cooperation with other local, state, and federal partner agencies. This program also buys land, easements, and rights-of-way  

[3:2:66,89] for federal flood control projects and supervises the design and construction of new dams and periodic inspection and  

[3:3:66,99] reevaluation of all existing jurisdictional dams for proper operation and maintenance. Fiscal oversight and coordination activities  

[3:4:66,109] associated with the Disaster Preparedness and Flood Prevention Bond Act of 2006 are administered under this program. The  

[3:5:66,120] program also reviews federal dam projects in coordination with federal and other state agencies with regard to dam safety.  

[3:6:66,137] 3250 - CENTRAL VALLEY FLOOD PROTECTION BOARD  

[4:0:66,155] The Central Valley Flood Protection Board has regulatory authority over the State Plan of Flood Control facilities (SPFC),  

[4:1:66,165] designated floodways and regulated streams in the Central Valley. The Board regulates encroachments on the system by  

[4:2:66,175] issuing permits and initiating enforcement action when necessary to maintain the integrity of the levees and floodways that  

[4:3:66,186] protect the valley's people and property. The Board manages the state's portfolio of real property held by the Sacramento-San  

[4:4:66,196] Joaquin Drainage District. The Board serves as the non-federal sponsor to the United States Army Corps of Engineers on large  

[4:5:66,206] joint state-federal levee improvement projects and assists the more than one hundred local maintaining agencies that operate  

[4:6:66,216] and maintain the SPFC. The Board conducts regular public meetings, workshops and tours, providing a public forum for  

[4:7:66,226] stakeholders.  

[4:8:66,244] 3255 - SERVICES  

[5:0:66,262] This program provides technical support within the Department and expertise in the fields of water resources planning,  

[5:1:66,272] development and management; watermaster services; scientific analyses performed by DWR's chemical laboratory;  

[5:2:66,282] information technology; and mapping, surveying and engineering services for other agencies.  

[5:3:66,300] 3260 - CALIFORNIA ENERGY RESOURCES SCHEDULING  

[6:0:66,320] For a limited period of time, this program purchased electric power on behalf of the state's investor-owned utilities. Beginning  

[6:1:66,331] January 1, 2003, the utility companies resumed responsibility for purchasing power from the spot market. The utilities, however,  

[6:2:66,341] continued to receive power from the Department's long-term energy contracts with energy suppliers, under which the  

[6:3:66,351] Department retains legal and financial responsibility. All energy contracts signed by the Department have now expired or were  

[6:4:66,361] terminated. However, litigation continues against some of the counterparties to these contracts. Additionally, the Department  

[6:5:66,371] retains the legal and financial responsibility for administering $3.213 billion in revenue bonds issued to repay the General Fund  

[6:6:66,382] for money borrowed for power purchases during the energy crisis and funding of reserve accounts necessary to maintain an  

[6:7:66,392] investment grade credit rating associated with the revenue bonds.

[7:0:66,279] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW  

[8:0:66,297] The Department of Water Resources has two major infrastructure programs: the State Water Project and the flood control  

[8:1:66,308] system in the Central Valley. The State Water Project is a water storage and delivery system that consists of 34 storage  

[8:2:66,318] facilities, reservoirs, and lakes, 21 pumping plants, 4 pumping-generating plants, 5 hydroelectric power plants, and over 700  

[8:3:66,328] miles of canals, pipelines, and tunnels. The Project provides water to 25 million Californians and 750,000 acres of irrigated  

[8:4:66,338] farmland. The Project also provides recreational opportunities to approximately 6 million visitors annually at 15 separate  

[8:5:66,348] reservoir-based facilities as well as public fishing access to much of the Aqueduct. Existing flood control infrastructure in the  

[8:6:66,359] Central Valley consists of 1,595 miles of levees, 348,000 acres of channels and floodways, over 800,000 linear feet of bank  

[8:7:66,369] protection, over 60 mitigation and environmental restoration sites, and 55 various flood control structures including dams, weirs,  

[8:8:66,379] pumping plants, diversion structures, gate structures, and drop structures. In general, these facilities provide more flood  

[8:9:66,389] protection in urbanized areas and less in agricultural areas.

[1:0:159,43] 3875   Sacramento-San Joaquin Delta Conservancy  

[2:0:66,64] The mission of the Sacramento-San Joaquin Delta Conservancy is to support efforts that advance both environmental  

[2:1:66,74] protection and the economic well-being of Delta residents in a complementary manner. The Conservancy's activities include  

[2:2:66,84] protecting and enhancing habitat and habitat restoration; protecting and preserving Delta agriculture and working landscapes;  

[2:3:66,95] providing increased opportunities for tourism and recreation; promoting Delta legacy communities and economic vitality in the  

[2:4:66,105] Delta; increasing the resilience of the Delta to the effects of climate change and natural disasters such as floods and  

[2:5:66,115] earthquakes; protecting and improving water quality; assisting the Delta regional economy; identifying priority projects and  

[2:6:66,125] initiatives for which funding is needed; conducting activities to protect, conserve, and restore the region's physical, agricultural,  

[2:7:66,135] cultural, historical, and living resources; assisting local entities in the implementation of their habitat conservation plans and  

[2:8:66,146] natural community conservation plans; facilitating protection and safe harbor agreements under the federal Endangered  

[2:9:66,156] Species Act of 1973 and the California Endangered Species Act for adjacent land owners and local public agencies; and  

[2:10:66,166] promoting environmental education. The Conservancy acts as a primary state agency to implement ecosystem restoration in  

[2:11:66,176] the Delta and supports efforts that advance environmental protection and the economic well-being of the Delta residents.

[0:0:66,13] NATURAL RESOURCES   2019-20 STATE BUDGET — RES   249 

[1:0:211,43] 3885   Delta Stewardship Council  

[2:0:66,64] Established in 2009 by the Delta Reform Act, the mission of the Delta Stewardship Council, through a seven-member board, is  

[2:1:66,74] to further the state's coequal goals of providing a more reliable water supply for California and protecting, restoring, and  

[2:2:66,84] enhancing the Sacramento-San Joaquin Delta ecosystem. The coequal goals are to be achieved in a manner that protects and  

[2:3:66,95] enhances the unique cultural, recreational, natural resource, and agricultural values of the Delta. In 2013, the Council adopted  

[2:4:66,105] a legally enforceable Delta Plan to further the coequal goals and guide state and local agency activities related to the Delta.  

[2:5:66,115] Under state law, agencies are required to coordinate their actions pursuant to the Delta Plan with the Council and the other  

[2:6:66,125] relevant agencies. The Council is informed by scientific input from the Delta Science Program and the Delta Independent  

[2:7:66,135] Science Board. The mission of the Delta Science Program is to provide the best possible unbiased scientific information to  

[2:8:66,146] inform water and environmental decisionmaking in the Delta.
